<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Fungi" redirects here. <p>A <b>fungus</b> (<a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a>: <b>fungi</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> or <b>funguses</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OxfordDictionary_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordDictionary-3">[3]</a></sup>) is any member of the group of <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotic</a> organisms that includes microorganisms such as <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeasts</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mold_(fungus)" title="Mold (fungus)">molds</a>, as well as the more familiar <a href="/wiki/Mushroom" title="Mushroom">mushrooms</a>. These organisms are classified as a <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">kingdom</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> separately from the other eukaryotic kingdoms, which by one traditional classification include <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">Plantae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">Animalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">Protozoa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chromista" title="Chromista">Chromista</a>.
</p><p>A characteristic that places fungi in a different kingdom from plants, bacteria, and some protists is <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a> in their <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell walls</a>. Fungi, like animals, are <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">heterotrophs</a>; they acquire their food by absorbing dissolved molecules, typically by secreting <a href="/wiki/Digestive_enzyme" title="Digestive enzyme">digestive enzymes</a> into their environment. Fungi do not <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesize</a>. Growth is their means of <a href="/wiki/Motility" title="Motility">mobility</a>, except for <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a> (a few of which are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flagellated_cell" title="Flagellated cell">flagellated</a>), which may travel through the air or water. Fungi are the principal <a href="/wiki/Decomposer" title="Decomposer">decomposers</a> in ecological systems. These and other differences place fungi in a single group of related organisms, named the <i>Eumycota</i> (<i>true fungi</i> or <i>Eumycetes</i>), that share a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_ancestor" title="Common ancestor">common ancestor</a> (i.e. they form a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a> group</i>), an interpretation that is also strongly supported by <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">molecular phylogenetics</a>. This fungal group is distinct from the structurally similar <a href="/wiki/Mycetozoa" title="Mycetozoa">myxomycetes</a> (slime molds) and <a href="/wiki/Oomycete" title="Oomycete">oomycetes</a> (water molds). The discipline of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> devoted to the study of fungi is known as <a href="/wiki/Mycology" title="Mycology">mycology</a> (from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> μύκης <i><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">mykes</i></span></i>, mushroom). In the past, mycology was regarded as a branch of <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botany</a>, although it is now known fungi are genetically more closely related to animals than to plants.
</p><p>Abundant worldwide, most fungi are inconspicuous because of the small size of their structures, and their <a href="/wiki/Crypsis" title="Crypsis">cryptic</a> lifestyles in soil or on dead matter. Fungi include <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbionts</a> of plants, animals, or other fungi and also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasites" title="Parasites">parasites</a>. They may become noticeable when <a href="/wiki/Sporocarp_(fungi)" title="Sporocarp (fungi)">fruiting</a>, either as mushrooms or as molds. Fungi perform an essential role in the decomposition of organic matter and have fundamental roles in nutrient <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">cycling</a> and exchange in the environment. They have long been used as a direct source of human food, in the form of mushrooms and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuber_(genus)" title="Tuber (genus)">truffles</a>; as a <a href="/wiki/Bread#Leavening" title="Bread">leavening</a> agent for bread; and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermentation</a> of various food products, such as <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>. Since the 1940s, fungi have been used for the production of <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a>, and, more recently, various <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> produced by fungi are used <a href="/wiki/Enzyme#Industrial_applications" title="Enzyme">industrially</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Protease#Occurrence" title="Protease">detergents</a>. Fungi are also used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_pesticide" title="Biological pesticide">biological pesticides</a> to control weeds, plant diseases and insect pests. Many species produce <a href="/wiki/Bioactive_compound" title="Bioactive compound">bioactive</a> compounds called <a href="/wiki/Mycotoxin" title="Mycotoxin">mycotoxins</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Alkaloid" title="Alkaloid">alkaloids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polyketide" title="Polyketide">polyketides</a>, that are toxic to animals including humans. The fruiting structures of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychoactive_mushrooms" title="Psychoactive mushrooms">a few species</a> contain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychotropic" title="Psychotropic">psychotropic</a> compounds and are consumed <a href="/wiki/Recreational_drug_use" title="Recreational drug use">recreationally</a> or in traditional <a href="/wiki/Entheogen" title="Entheogen">spiritual ceremonies</a>. Fungi can break down manufactured materials and buildings, and become significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pathogenic_fungi" title="Pathogenic fungi">pathogens</a> of humans and other animals. Losses of crops due to fungal diseases (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rice_blast_disease" title="Rice blast disease">rice blast disease</a>) or food <a href="/wiki/Food_spoilage" title="Food spoilage">spoilage</a> can have a large impact on human <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food supplies</a> and local economies.
</p><p>The fungus kingdom encompasses an enormous diversity of <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxa</a> with varied ecologies, <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">life cycle</a> strategies, and <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphologies</a> ranging from unicellular aquatic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chytrid" title="Chytrid">chytrids</a> to large mushrooms. However, little is known of the true <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> of Kingdom Fungi, which has been estimated at 2.2 million to 3.8 million species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lucking2017_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lucking2017-5">[5]</a></sup> Of these, only about 148,000 have been described,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cheek_et_al._2020_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cheek_et_al._2020-6">[6]</a></sup> with over 8,000 species known to be detrimental to plants and at least 300 that can be pathogenic to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Ever since the pioneering 18th and 19th century <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomical</a> works of <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Hendrik_Persoon" title="Christiaan Hendrik Persoon">Christiaan Hendrik Persoon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Elias_Magnus_Fries" title="Elias Magnus Fries">Elias Magnus Fries</a>, fungi have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_classification" title="Biological classification">classified</a> according to their morphology (e.g., characteristics such as spore color or microscopic features) or <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>. Advances in <a href="/wiki/Molecular_genetics" title="Molecular genetics">molecular genetics</a> have opened the way for <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequencing" title="DNA sequencing">DNA analysis</a> to be incorporated into taxonomy, which has sometimes challenged the historical groupings based on morphology and other traits. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogenetic" title="Phylogenetic">Phylogenetic</a> studies published in the first decade of the 21st century have helped reshape the classification within Kingdom Fungi, which is divided into one <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)#Definition_and_associated_terms" title="Kingdom (biology)">subkingdom</a>, seven <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a>, and ten subphyla.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The English word <i>fungus</i> is directly adopted from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>fungus</i> (mushroom), used in the writings of <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simpson1979_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simpson1979-8">[8]</a></sup> This in turn is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> word <i>sphongos</i> (σφόγγος 'sponge'), which refers to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macroscopic" title="Macroscopic">macroscopic</a> structures and morphology of mushrooms and molds;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ainsworth2_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ainsworth2-9">[9]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">root</a> is also used in other languages, such as the German <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Schwamm#German" title="wikt:Schwamm">Schwamm</a></i> ('sponge') and <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Schwamm#German" title="wikt:Schwamm">Schimmel</a></i> ('mold').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitzka1960_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mitzka1960-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The word <i><a href="/wiki/Mycology" title="Mycology">mycology</a></i> is derived from the Greek <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">mykes</i></span> (μύκης 'mushroom') and <i>logos</i> (λόγος 'discourse').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> It denotes the scientific study of fungi. The Latin adjectival form of "mycology" (<i>mycologicæ</i>) appeared as early as 1796 in a book on the subject by <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Hendrik_Persoon" title="Christiaan Hendrik Persoon">Christiaan Hendrik Persoon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The word appeared in English as early as 1824 in a book by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kaye_Greville" title="Robert Kaye Greville">Robert Kaye Greville</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In 1836 the English naturalist <a href="/wiki/Miles_Joseph_Berkeley" title="Miles Joseph Berkeley">Miles Joseph Berkeley</a>'s publication <i>The English Flora of Sir James Edward Smith, Vol. 5.</i> also refers to mycology as the study of fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ainsworth2_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ainsworth2-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>A group of all the fungi present in a particular region is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobiota" title="Mycobiota">mycobiota</a></i> (plural noun, no singular).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The term <i>mycota</i> is often used for this purpose, but many authors use it as a synonym of Fungi. The word <i><a href="/wiki/Funga" title="Funga">funga</a></i> has been proposed as a less ambiguous term morphologically similar to <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">flora</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhar_et_al._2018_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhar_et_al._2018-16">[16]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Species_Survival_Commission" title="Species Survival Commission">Species Survival Commission</a> (SSC) of the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN) in August 2021 asked that the phrase <i>fauna and flora</i> be replaced by <i>fauna, flora, and funga</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HYPHAE.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="721" data-file-width="1364" decoding="async" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/HYPHAE.png/220px-HYPHAE.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/HYPHAE.png/330px-HYPHAE.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/HYPHAE.png/440px-HYPHAE.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HYPHAE.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>Fungal hyphae cells</b> <div class="hlist hlist-separated"><div><ol><li>Hyphal wall</li><li><a href="/wiki/Septum" title="Septum">Septum</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">Mitochondrion</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Vacuole" title="Vacuole">Vacuole</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Ergosterol" title="Ergosterol">Ergosterol</a> crystal</li><li><a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">Ribosome</a></li><li>Nucleus</li><li><a href="/wiki/Endoplasmic_reticulum" title="Endoplasmic reticulum">Endoplasmic reticulum</a></li><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipid_body" title="Lipid body">Lipid body</a></li><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_membrane" title="Plasma membrane">Plasma membrane</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Spitzenk%C3%B6rper" title="Spitzenkörper">Spitzenkörper</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Golgi_apparatus" title="Golgi apparatus">Golgi apparatus</a></li></ol></div> </div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fungus_cell_cycle-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1491" data-file-width="2310" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Fungus_cell_cycle-en.svg/220px-Fungus_cell_cycle-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Fungus_cell_cycle-en.svg/330px-Fungus_cell_cycle-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Fungus_cell_cycle-en.svg/440px-Fungus_cell_cycle-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fungus_cell_cycle-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fungal cell cycle showing <a href="/wiki/Dikaryon" title="Dikaryon">Dikaryons</a> typical of <a href="/wiki/Dikarya" title="Dikarya">Higher Fungi</a></div></div></div>
<p>Before the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">molecular methods</a> for phylogenetic analysis, <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomists</a> considered fungi to be members of the <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plant kingdom</a> because of similarities in lifestyle: both fungi and plants are mainly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sessility_(zoology)" title="Sessility (zoology)">immobile</a>, and have similarities in general morphology and growth habitat. Although inaccurate, the common misconception that fungi are plants persists among the general public due to their historical classification, as well as several similarities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Like plants, fungi often grow in soil and, in the case of <a href="/wiki/Mushroom" title="Mushroom">mushrooms</a>, form conspicuous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fruit_bodies" title="Fruit bodies">fruit bodies</a>, which sometimes resemble plants such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosses" title="Mosses">mosses</a>. The fungi are now considered a separate kingdom, distinct from both plants and animals, from which they appear to have <a href="/wiki/Genetic_divergence" title="Genetic divergence">diverged</a> around one billion years ago (around the start of the <a href="/wiki/Neoproterozoic" title="Neoproterozoic">Neoproterozoic</a> Era).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruns2006_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruns2006-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baldauf1993_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baldauf1993-21">[21]</a></sup> Some morphological, biochemical, and genetic features are shared with other organisms, while others are unique to the fungi, clearly separating them from the other kingdoms:
</p><p>Shared features:
</p>
<ul><li>With other <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a>: Fungal cells contain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Membrane-bound" title="Membrane-bound">membrane-bound</a> <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nuclei</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromosomes" title="Chromosomes">chromosomes</a> that contain <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noncoding_DNA" title="Noncoding DNA">noncoding regions</a> called <a href="/wiki/Intron" title="Intron">introns</a> and coding regions called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exons" title="Exons">exons</a>. Fungi have membrane-bound cytoplasmic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organelles" title="Organelles">organelles</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sterol" title="Sterol">sterol</a>-containing membranes, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ribosomes" title="Ribosomes">ribosomes</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/80S" title="80S">80S</a> type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> They have a characteristic range of soluble carbohydrates and storage compounds, including <a href="/wiki/Sugar_alcohol" title="Sugar alcohol">sugar alcohols</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Mannitol" title="Mannitol">mannitol</a>), <a href="/wiki/Disaccharide" title="Disaccharide">disaccharides</a>, (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Trehalose" title="Trehalose">trehalose</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>, which is also found in animals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deacon4_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deacon4-23">[23]</a></sup>).</li>
<li>With animals: Fungi lack <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a> and are <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">heterotrophic</a> organisms and so require preformed <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> as energy sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></li>
<li>With plants: Fungi have a cell wall<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Vacuole" title="Vacuole">vacuoles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shoji2006_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shoji2006-26">[26]</a></sup> They reproduce by both sexual and asexual means, and like <a href="/wiki/Primitive_(phylogenetics)" title="Primitive (phylogenetics)">basal</a> plant groups (such as <a href="/wiki/Fern" title="Fern">ferns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a>) produce <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a>. Similar to mosses and algae, fungi typically have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haploid" title="Haploid">haploid</a> nuclei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deacon58_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deacon58-27">[27]</a></sup></li>
<li>With <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euglenoid" title="Euglenoid">euglenoids</a> and bacteria: Higher fungi, euglenoids, and some bacteria produce the <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> <small>L</small>-lysine in specific <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">biosynthesis</a> steps, called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%91-aminoadipate_pathway" title="Α-aminoadipate pathway">α-aminoadipate pathway</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zabriskie2000_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zabriskie2000-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xu2006_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xu2006-29">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li>The cells of most fungi grow as tubular, elongated, and thread-like (filamentous) structures called <a href="/wiki/Hypha" title="Hypha">hyphae</a>, which may contain multiple nuclei and extend by growing at their tips. Each tip contains a set of aggregated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesicle_(biology)" title="Vesicle (biology)">vesicles</a>—cellular structures consisting of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, and other organic molecules—called the <a href="/wiki/Spitzenk%C3%B6rper" title="Spitzenkörper">Spitzenkörper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Both fungi and <a href="/wiki/Oomycete" title="Oomycete">oomycetes</a> grow as filamentous hyphal cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In contrast, similar-looking organisms, such as filamentous <a href="/wiki/Green_algae" title="Green algae">green algae</a>, grow by repeated cell division within a chain of cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deacon4_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Deacon4-23">[23]</a></sup> There are also single-celled fungi (<a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeasts</a>) that do not form hyphae, and some fungi have both hyphal and yeast forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexopoulos_et_al.,_p._30_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alexopoulos_et_al.,_p._30-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>In common with some plant and animal species, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_bioluminescent_fungi" title="List of bioluminescent fungi">more than 70 fungal species</a> display <a href="/wiki/Bioluminescence" title="Bioluminescence">bioluminescence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Desjardin_2010_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Desjardin_2010-33">[33]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Unique features:
</p>
<ul><li>Some species grow as unicellular yeasts that reproduce by <a href="/wiki/Budding" title="Budding">budding</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_fission" title="Binary fission">fission</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dimorphic_fungi" title="Dimorphic fungi">Dimorphic fungi</a> can switch between a yeast phase and a hyphal phase in response to environmental conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexopoulos_et_al.,_p._30_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alexopoulos_et_al.,_p._30-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>The fungal cell wall is made of a <a href="/wiki/Chitin-glucan_complex" title="Chitin-glucan complex">chitin-glucan complex</a>; while glucans are also found in plants and chitin in the <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeleton</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arthropods" title="Arthropods">arthropods</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> fungi are the only organisms that combine these two structural molecules in their cell wall. Unlike those of plants and oomycetes, fungal cell walls do not contain cellulose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gow_et_al._2017_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gow_et_al._2017-36">[36]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Omphalotus_nidiformis_Binnamittalong_2_email.jpg"><img alt="A whitish fan or funnel-shaped mushroom growing at the base of a tree." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1088" data-file-width="1152" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Omphalotus_nidiformis_Binnamittalong_2_email.jpg/220px-Omphalotus_nidiformis_Binnamittalong_2_email.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Omphalotus_nidiformis_Binnamittalong_2_email.jpg/330px-Omphalotus_nidiformis_Binnamittalong_2_email.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Omphalotus_nidiformis_Binnamittalong_2_email.jpg/440px-Omphalotus_nidiformis_Binnamittalong_2_email.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Omphalotus_nidiformis_Binnamittalong_2_email.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Omphalotus_nidiformis" title="Omphalotus nidiformis">Omphalotus nidiformis</a></i>, a bioluminescent mushroom</div></div></div>
<p>Most fungi lack an efficient system for the long-distance transport of water and nutrients, such as the <a href="/wiki/Xylem" title="Xylem">xylem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phloem" title="Phloem">phloem</a> in many plants. To overcome this limitation, some fungi, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Armillaria" title="Armillaria">Armillaria</a></i>, form <a href="/wiki/Mycelial_cord" title="Mycelial cord">rhizomorphs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mikhail2005_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mikhail2005-37">[37]</a></sup> which resemble and perform functions similar to the <a href="/wiki/Root" title="Root">roots</a> of plants. As eukaryotes, fungi possess a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biochemical_pathway" title="Biochemical pathway">biosynthetic pathway</a> for producing <a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">terpenes</a> that uses <a href="/wiki/Mevalonic_acid" title="Mevalonic acid">mevalonic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyrophosphate" title="Pyrophosphate">pyrophosphate</a> as <a href="/wiki/Precursor_(chemistry)" title="Precursor (chemistry)">chemical building blocks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keller2005_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keller2005-38">[38]</a></sup> Plants and some other organisms have an additional terpene biosynthesis pathway in their chloroplasts, a structure that fungi and animals do not have.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu2007_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wu2007-39">[39]</a></sup> Fungi produce several <a href="/wiki/Secondary_metabolite" title="Secondary metabolite">secondary metabolites</a> that are similar or identical in structure to those made by plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keller2005_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keller2005-38">[38]</a></sup> Many of the plant and fungal enzymes that make these compounds differ from each other in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peptide_sequence" title="Peptide sequence">sequence</a> and other characteristics, which indicates separate origins and <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a> of these enzymes in the fungi and plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keller2005_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Keller2005-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tudzynski2005_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tudzynski2005-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity">Diversity</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fungus_in_a_Wood.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Fungus_in_a_Wood.JPG/220px-Fungus_in_a_Wood.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Fungus_in_a_Wood.JPG/330px-Fungus_in_a_Wood.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Fungus_in_a_Wood.JPG/440px-Fungus_in_a_Wood.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fungus_in_a_Wood.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bracket_fungus" title="Bracket fungus">Bracket fungi</a> on a tree stump</div></div></div>
<p>Fungi have a worldwide distribution, and grow in a wide range of habitats, including extreme environments such as <a href="/wiki/Desert_fungi" title="Desert fungi">deserts</a> or areas with high salt concentrations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaupotic2008_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaupotic2008-41">[41]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dadachova2007_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dadachova2007-42">[42]</a></sup> as well as in <a href="/wiki/Deep_sea" title="Deep sea">deep sea</a> sediments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raghukumar1998_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raghukumar1998-43">[43]</a></sup> Some can survive the intense <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" title="Ultraviolet radiation">UV</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_radiation" title="Cosmic radiation">cosmic radiation</a> encountered during space travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sancho2007_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sancho2007-44">[44]</a></sup> Most grow in terrestrial environments, though several species live partly or solely in aquatic habitats, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chytrid" title="Chytrid">chytrid</a> fungi <i><a href="/wiki/Batrachochytrium_dendrobatidis" title="Batrachochytrium dendrobatidis">Batrachochytrium dendrobatidis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Batrachochytrium_salamandrivorans" title="Batrachochytrium salamandrivorans">B. salamandrivorans</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a> that have been responsible for a worldwide decline in <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibian</a> populations. These organisms spend part of their life cycle as a motile <a href="/wiki/Zoospore" title="Zoospore">zoospore</a>, enabling them to propel itself through water and enter their amphibian host.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fisher_et_al._2020_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fisher_et_al._2020-45">[45]</a></sup> Other examples of aquatic fungi include those living in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrothermal" title="Hydrothermal">hydrothermal</a> areas of the ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vargas-Gastélum_&amp;_Riquelme_2020_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vargas-Gastélum_&amp;_Riquelme_2020-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2020, around 148,000 species of fungi have been <a href="/wiki/Species_description" title="Species description">described</a> by <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomists</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cheek_et_al._2020_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cheek_et_al._2020-6">[6]</a></sup> but the global biodiversity of the fungus kingdom is not fully understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mueller2006_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller2006-47">[47]</a></sup> A 2017 estimate suggests there may be between 2.2 and 3.8 million species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lucking2017_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lucking2017-5">[5]</a></sup> The number of new fungi species discovered yearly has increased from 1,000 to 1,500 per year about 10 years ago, to about 2000 with a peak of more than 2,500 species in 2016. In the year 2019, 1882 new species of fungi were described, and it was estimated that more than 90% of fungi remain unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cheek_et_al._2020_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cheek_et_al._2020-6">[6]</a></sup> The following year, 2905 new species were described—the highest annual record of new fungus names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_et_al._2021_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang_et_al._2021-48">[48]</a></sup> In mycology, species have historically been distinguished by a variety of methods and concepts. Classification based on <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> characteristics, such as the size and shape of spores or fruiting structures, has traditionally dominated fungal taxonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirk_et_al.,_p._489_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirk_et_al.,_p._489-49">[49]</a></sup> Species may also be distinguished by their <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiological</a> characteristics, such as their ability to metabolize certain biochemicals, or their reaction to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_tests_in_mushroom_identification" title="Chemical tests in mushroom identification">chemical tests</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Species#The_isolation_species_concept_in_more_detail" title="Species">biological species concept</a> discriminates species based on their ability to <a href="/wiki/Mating_in_fungi" title="Mating in fungi">mate</a>. The application of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular</a> tools, such as <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequencing" title="DNA sequencing">DNA sequencing</a> and phylogenetic analysis, to study diversity has greatly enhanced the resolution and added robustness to estimates of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> within various taxonomic groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hibbett2007_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hibbett2007-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mycology">Mycology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pier_Antonio_Micheli.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="428" decoding="async" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Pier_Antonio_Micheli.jpg/170px-Pier_Antonio_Micheli.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Pier_Antonio_Micheli.jpg/255px-Pier_Antonio_Micheli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Pier_Antonio_Micheli.jpg/340px-Pier_Antonio_Micheli.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pier_Antonio_Micheli.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1729, <a href="/wiki/Pier_Antonio_Micheli" title="Pier Antonio Micheli">Pier Antonio Micheli</a> first published descriptions of fungi.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mycology" title="Mycology">Mycology</a> is the branch of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> concerned with the systematic study of fungi, including their genetic and biochemical properties, their taxonomy, and their use to humans as a source of medicine, food, and <a href="/wiki/Entheogen" title="Entheogen">psychotropic substances</a> consumed for religious purposes, as well as their dangers, such as poisoning or infection. The field of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phytopathology" title="Phytopathology">phytopathology</a>, the study of plant diseases, is closely related because many plant pathogens are fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Struck2006_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Struck2006-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of fungi by humans dates back to prehistory; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%96tzi_the_Iceman" title="Ötzi the Iceman">Ötzi the Iceman</a>, a well-preserved mummy of a 5,300-year-old <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> man found frozen in the Austrian Alps, carried two species of <a href="/wiki/Polypore" title="Polypore">polypore</a> mushrooms that may have been used as <a href="/wiki/Tinder" title="Tinder">tinder</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Fomes_fomentarius" title="Fomes fomentarius">Fomes fomentarius</a></i>), or for medicinal purposes (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piptoporus_betulinus" title="Piptoporus betulinus">Piptoporus betulinus</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peintner1998_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peintner1998-52">[52]</a></sup> Ancient peoples have used fungi as food sources—often unknowingly—for millennia, in the preparation of leavened bread and fermented juices. Some of the oldest written records contain references to the destruction of crops that were probably caused by pathogenic fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h3>
<p>Mycology became a systematic science after the development of the <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscope</a> in the 17th century. Although fungal spores were first observed by <a href="/wiki/Giambattista_della_Porta" title="Giambattista della Porta">Giambattista della Porta</a> in 1588, the seminal work in the development of mycology is considered to be the publication of <a href="/wiki/Pier_Antonio_Micheli" title="Pier Antonio Micheli">Pier Antonio Micheli</a>'s 1729 work <i>Nova plantarum genera</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Micheli not only observed spores but also showed that, under the proper conditions, they could be induced into growing into the same species of fungi from which they originated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Extending the use of the <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial system of nomenclature</a> introduced by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Species_plantarum" title="Species plantarum">Species plantarum</a></i> (1753), the Dutch <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Hendrik_Persoon" title="Christiaan Hendrik Persoon">Christiaan Hendrik Persoon</a> (1761–1836) established the first classification of mushrooms with such skill as to be considered a founder of modern mycology. Later, <a href="/wiki/Elias_Magnus_Fries" title="Elias Magnus Fries">Elias Magnus Fries</a> (1794–1878) further elaborated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_classification" title="Biological classification">classification</a> of fungi, using spore color and microscopic characteristics, methods still used by taxonomists today. Other notable early contributors to mycology in the 17th–19th and early 20th centuries include <a href="/wiki/Miles_Joseph_Berkeley" title="Miles Joseph Berkeley">Miles Joseph Berkeley</a>, <a href="/wiki/August_Carl_Joseph_Corda" title="August Carl Joseph Corda">August Carl Joseph Corda</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anton_de_Bary" title="Anton de Bary">Anton de Bary</a>, the brothers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_Ren%C3%A9_Tulasne" title="Louis René Tulasne">Louis René</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Tulasne" title="Charles Tulasne">Charles Tulasne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Henry_Reginald_Buller" title="Arthur Henry Reginald Buller">Arthur H. R. Buller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Curtis_Gates_Lloyd" title="Curtis Gates Lloyd">Curtis G. Lloyd</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pier_Andrea_Saccardo" title="Pier Andrea Saccardo">Pier Andrea Saccardo</a>. In the 20th and 21st centuries, advances in <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequencing" title="DNA sequencing">DNA sequencing</a> and phylogenetic analysis has provided new insights into fungal relationships and <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>, and has challenged traditional morphology-based groupings in fungal <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawksworth2006_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawksworth2006-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microscopic_structures">Microscopic structures</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Penicillium_labeled_cropped.jpg"><img alt="Monochrome micrograph showing Penicillium hyphae as long, transparent, tube-like structures a few micrometres across. Conidiophores branch out laterally from the hyphae, terminating in bundles of phialides on which spherical condidiophores are arranged like beads on a string. Septa are faintly visible as dark lines crossing the hyphae." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="648" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Penicillium_labeled_cropped.jpg/330px-Penicillium_labeled_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Penicillium_labeled_cropped.jpg/495px-Penicillium_labeled_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/30/Penicillium_labeled_cropped.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Penicillium_labeled_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An environmental isolate of <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium" title="Penicillium">Penicillium</a></i> <div class="hlist hlist-separated"><div><ol><li><a href="/wiki/Hypha" title="Hypha">hypha</a></li><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conidiophore" title="Conidiophore">conidiophore</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Phialide" title="Phialide">phialide</a></li><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conidia" title="Conidia">conidia</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Septum" title="Septum">septa</a></li></ol></div> </div></div></div></div>
<p>Most fungi grow as <a href="/wiki/Hypha" title="Hypha">hyphae</a>, which are cylindrical, thread-like structures 2–10<span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrometres" title="Micrometres">µm</a> in diameter and up to several centimeters in length. Hyphae grow at their tips (apices); new hyphae are typically formed by emergence of new tips along existing hyphae by a process called <i>branching</i>, or occasionally growing hyphal tips fork, giving rise to two parallel-growing hyphae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris2008_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris2008-57">[57]</a></sup> Hyphae also sometimes fuse when they come into contact, a process called hyphal fusion (or <a href="/wiki/Anastomosis" title="Anastomosis">anastomosis</a>). These growth processes lead to the development of a <a href="/wiki/Mycelium" title="Mycelium">mycelium</a>, an interconnected network of hyphae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexopoulos_et_al.,_p._30_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Alexopoulos_et_al.,_p._30-32">[32]</a></sup> Hyphae can be either <a href="/wiki/Septum" title="Septum">septate</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coenocytic" title="Coenocytic">coenocytic</a>. Septate hyphae are divided into compartments separated by cross walls (internal cell walls, called septa, that are formed at <a href="/wiki/Right_angle" title="Right angle">right angles</a> to the cell wall giving the hypha its shape), with each compartment containing one or more nuclei; coenocytic hyphae are not compartmentalized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Septa have <a href="/wiki/Pit_connection#Characteristics" title="Pit connection">pores</a> that allow <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a>, and sometimes nuclei to pass through; an example is the <a href="/wiki/Dolipore_septum" title="Dolipore septum">dolipore septum</a> in fungi of the phylum Basidiomycota.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Coenocytic hyphae are in essence <a href="/wiki/Multinucleate" title="Multinucleate">multinucleate</a> supercells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang2004_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chang2004-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many species have developed specialized hyphal structures for nutrient uptake from living hosts; examples include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haustoria" title="Haustoria">haustoria</a> in plant-parasitic species of most fungal phyla,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bozkurt_et_al._2020_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bozkurt_et_al._2020-61">[61]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Arbuscular_mycorrhiza" title="Arbuscular mycorrhiza">arbuscules</a> of several <a href="/wiki/Mycorrhiza" title="Mycorrhiza">mycorrhizal</a> fungi, which penetrate into the host cells to consume nutrients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parniske2008_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parniske2008-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although fungi are <a href="/wiki/Opisthokont" title="Opisthokont">opisthokonts</a>—a grouping of evolutionarily related organisms broadly characterized by a single posterior <a href="/wiki/Flagellum" title="Flagellum">flagellum</a>—all phyla except for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chytrids" title="Chytrids">chytrids</a> have lost their posterior flagella.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steenkamp2006_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steenkamp2006-63">[63]</a></sup> Fungi are unusual among the eukaryotes in having a cell wall that, in addition to <a href="/wiki/Glucan" title="Glucan">glucans</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Beta-glucan" title="Beta-glucan">β-1,3-glucan</a>) and other typical components, also contains the <a href="/wiki/Biopolymer" title="Biopolymer">biopolymer</a> chitin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gow_et_al._2017_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gow_et_al._2017-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Macroscopic_structures">Macroscopic structures</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Armillaria_ostoyae_MO.jpg"><img alt="A cluster of large, thick-stem, light-brown gilled mushrooms growing at the base of a tree" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Armillaria_ostoyae_MO.jpg/220px-Armillaria_ostoyae_MO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Armillaria_ostoyae_MO.jpg/330px-Armillaria_ostoyae_MO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Armillaria_ostoyae_MO.jpg/440px-Armillaria_ostoyae_MO.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Armillaria_ostoyae_MO.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armillaria_solidipes" title="Armillaria solidipes">Armillaria solidipes</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Fungal mycelia can become visible to the naked eye, for example, on various surfaces and <a href="/wiki/Substrate_(biology)" title="Substrate (biology)">substrates</a>, such as damp walls and spoiled food, where they are commonly called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mold_(fungus)" title="Mold (fungus)">molds</a>. Mycelia grown on solid <a href="/wiki/Agar" title="Agar">agar</a> media in laboratory <a href="/wiki/Petri_dish" title="Petri dish">petri dishes</a> are usually referred to as <a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)" title="Colony (biology)">colonies</a>. These colonies can exhibit growth shapes and colors (due to spores or <a href="/wiki/Biological_pigment" title="Biological pigment">pigmentation</a>) that can be used as diagnostic features in the identification of species or groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Some individual fungal colonies can reach extraordinary dimensions and ages as in the case of a <a href="/wiki/Clone_(cell_biology)" title="Clone (cell biology)">clonal</a> colony of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armillaria_solidipes" title="Armillaria solidipes">Armillaria solidipes</a></i>, which extends over an area of more than 900<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">ha</a> (3.5 square miles), with an estimated age of nearly 9,000<span class="nowrap"> </span>years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferguson2003_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson2003-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apothecium" title="Apothecium">apothecium</a>—a specialized structure important in <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexual reproduction</a> in the ascomycetes—is a cup-shaped fruit body that is often macroscopic and holds the <a href="/wiki/Hymenium" title="Hymenium">hymenium</a>, a layer of tissue containing the spore-bearing cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The fruit bodies of the basidiomycetes (<a href="/wiki/Basidiocarp" title="Basidiocarp">basidiocarps</a>) and some ascomycetes can sometimes grow very large, and many are well known as <a href="/wiki/Mushroom" title="Mushroom">mushrooms</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_and_physiology">Growth and physiology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DecayingPeachSmall.gif"><img alt="Time-lapse photography sequence of a peach becoming progressively discolored and disfigured" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="190" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/DecayingPeachSmall.gif" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mold_(fungus)" title="Mold (fungus)">Mold</a> growth covering a decaying <a href="/wiki/Peach" title="Peach">peach</a>. The frames were taken approximately 12 hours apart over a period of six days.</div></div></div>
<p>The growth of fungi as hyphae on or in solid substrates or as single cells in aquatic environments is adapted for the efficient extraction of nutrients, because these growth forms have high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_area_to_volume_ratio" title="Surface area to volume ratio">surface area to volume ratios</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moss1986_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moss1986-67">[67]</a></sup> Hyphae are specifically adapted for growth on solid surfaces, and to invade <a href="/wiki/Substrate_(biology)" title="Substrate (biology)">substrates</a> and tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penalva2002_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Penalva2002-68">[68]</a></sup> They can exert large penetrative mechanical forces; for example, many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_pathogen" title="Plant pathogen">plant pathogens</a>, including <i><a href="/wiki/Magnaporthe_grisea" title="Magnaporthe grisea">Magnaporthe grisea</a></i>, form a structure called an <a href="/wiki/Appressorium" title="Appressorium">appressorium</a> that evolved to puncture plant tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howard1991_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howard1991-69">[69]</a></sup> The pressure generated by the appressorium, directed against the plant <a href="/wiki/Epidermis_(botany)" title="Epidermis (botany)">epidermis</a>, can exceed 8 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">megapascals</a> (1,200 psi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howard1991_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Howard1991-69">[69]</a></sup> The filamentous fungus <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paecilomyces_lilacinus" title="Paecilomyces lilacinus">Paecilomyces lilacinus</a></i> uses a similar structure to penetrate the eggs of <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Money1997_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Money1997-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>The mechanical pressure exerted by the appressorium is generated from physiological processes that increase intracellular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turgor" title="Turgor">turgor</a> by producing <a href="/wiki/Osmolyte" title="Osmolyte">osmolytes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang2005_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang2005-71">[71]</a></sup> Adaptations such as these are complemented by <a href="/wiki/Cellulase" title="Cellulase">hydrolytic enzymes</a> secreted into the environment to digest large organic molecules—such as <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>—into smaller molecules that may then be absorbed as nutrients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pereira2007_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pereira2007-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaller2007_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schaller2007-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farrar1985_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farrar1985-74">[74]</a></sup> The vast majority of filamentous fungi grow in a polar fashion (extending in one direction) by elongation at the tip (apex) of the hypha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer2008_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer2008-75">[75]</a></sup> Other forms of fungal growth include intercalary extension (longitudinal expansion of hyphal compartments that are below the apex) as in the case of some <a href="/wiki/Endophyte" title="Endophyte">endophytic</a> fungi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christensen2008_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christensen2008-76">[76]</a></sup> or growth by volume expansion during the development of mushroom <a href="/wiki/Stipe_(mycology)" title="Stipe (mycology)">stipes</a> and other large organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Money2002_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Money2002-77">[77]</a></sup> Growth of fungi as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellularity" title="Multicellularity">multicellular structures</a> consisting of <a href="/wiki/Somatic_(biology)" title="Somatic (biology)">somatic</a> and reproductive cells—a feature independently evolved in animals and plants<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willensdorfer2009_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Willensdorfer2009-78">[78]</a></sup>—has several functions, including the development of fruit bodies for dissemination of sexual spores (see above) and <a href="/wiki/Biofilm" title="Biofilm">biofilms</a> for substrate colonization and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intercellular_communication" title="Intercellular communication">intercellular communication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniels2006_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daniels2006-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>The fungi are traditionally considered <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">heterotrophs</a>, organisms that rely solely on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">carbon fixed</a> by other organisms for <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>. Fungi have <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolved</a> a high degree of metabolic versatility that allows them to use a diverse range of organic substrates for growth, including simple compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetate" title="Acetate">acetate</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tudzynski_2014_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tudzynski_2014-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heynes1994_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heynes1994-81">[81]</a></sup> In some species the pigment <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">melanin</a> may play a role in extracting energy from <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_rays" title="Gamma rays">gamma radiation</a>. This form of <a href="/wiki/Radiotrophic_fungus" title="Radiotrophic fungus">"radiotrophic"</a> growth has been described for only a few species, the effects on growth rates are small, and the underlying <a href="/wiki/Biophysics" title="Biophysics">biophysical</a> and biochemical processes are not well known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dadachova2007_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dadachova2007-42">[42]</a></sup> This process might bear similarity to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">CO<sub>2</sub> fixation</a> via <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible light</a>, but instead uses ionizing radiation as a source of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dadachova2008_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dadachova2008-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polyporus_squamosus_Molter.jpg"><img alt="Two thickly stemmed brownish mushrooms with scales on the upper surface, growing out of a tree trunk" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1959" data-file-width="2612" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Polyporus_squamosus_Molter.jpg/220px-Polyporus_squamosus_Molter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Polyporus_squamosus_Molter.jpg/330px-Polyporus_squamosus_Molter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Polyporus_squamosus_Molter.jpg/440px-Polyporus_squamosus_Molter.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polyporus_squamosus_Molter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyporus_squamosus" title="Polyporus squamosus">Polyporus squamosus</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Fungal reproduction is complex, reflecting the differences in lifestyles and genetic makeup within this diverse kingdom of organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> It is estimated that a third of all fungi reproduce using more than one method of propagation; for example, reproduction may occur in two well-differentiated stages within the <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">life cycle</a> of a species, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teleomorph" title="Teleomorph">teleomorph</a> (sexual reproduction) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anamorph" title="Anamorph">anamorph</a> (asexual reproduction).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Environmental conditions trigger genetically determined developmental states that lead to the creation of specialized structures for sexual or asexual reproduction. These structures aid reproduction by efficiently dispersing spores or spore-containing <a href="/wiki/Propagule" title="Propagule">propagules</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asexual_reproduction">Asexual reproduction</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">Asexual reproduction</a> occurs via vegetative spores (<a href="/wiki/Conidium" title="Conidium">conidia</a>) or through <a href="/wiki/Fragmentation_(reproduction)" title="Fragmentation (reproduction)">mycelial fragmentation</a>. Mycelial fragmentation occurs when a fungal mycelium separates into pieces, and each component grows into a separate mycelium. Mycelial fragmentation and vegetative spores maintain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clone_(genetics)" title="Clone (genetics)">clonal</a> populations adapted to a specific <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">niche</a>, and allow more rapid dispersal than sexual reproduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heitman2005_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heitman2005-85">[85]</a></sup> The "Fungi imperfecti" (fungi lacking the perfect or sexual stage) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuteromycota" title="Deuteromycota">Deuteromycota</a> comprise all the species that lack an observable sexual cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alcamo2004_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alcamo2004-86">[86]</a></sup> Deuteromycota (alternatively known as Deuteromycetes, conidial fungi, or mitosporic fungi) is not an accepted taxonomic clade and is now taken to mean simply fungi that lack a known sexual stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulloa_&amp;_Hanlin_2014_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ulloa_&amp;_Hanlin_2014-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_reproduction">Sexual reproduction</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mating_in_fungi" title="Mating in fungi">Mating in fungi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection_in_fungi" title="Sexual selection in fungi">Sexual selection in fungi</a></div>
<p>Sexual reproduction with <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a> has been directly observed in all fungal phyla except <a href="/wiki/Glomeromycota" title="Glomeromycota">Glomeromycota</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Redecker2006_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Redecker2006-88">[88]</a></sup> (genetic analysis suggests meiosis in Glomeromycota as well). It differs in many aspects from sexual reproduction in animals or plants. Differences also exist between fungal groups and can be used to discriminate species by morphological differences in sexual structures and reproductive strategies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guarro1999_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guarro1999-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2000_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2000-90">[90]</a></sup> Mating experiments between fungal isolates may identify species on the basis of biological species concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2000_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2000-90">[90]</a></sup> The major fungal groupings have initially been delineated based on the morphology of their sexual structures and spores; for example, the spore-containing structures, <a href="/wiki/Ascus" title="Ascus">asci</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basidium" title="Basidium">basidia</a>, can be used in the identification of ascomycetes and basidiomycetes, respectively. Fungi employ two <a href="/wiki/Mating_system" title="Mating system">mating systems</a>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterothallic" title="Heterothallic">heterothallic</a> species allow mating only between individuals of the opposite <a href="/wiki/Mating_type" title="Mating type">mating type</a>, whereas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homothallic" title="Homothallic">homothallic</a> species can mate, and sexually reproduce, with any other individual or itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metzenberg1990_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metzenberg1990-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most fungi have both a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haploid" title="Haploid">haploid</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">diploid</a> stage in their life cycles. In sexually reproducing fungi, compatible individuals may combine by fusing their hyphae together into an interconnected network; this process, <a href="/wiki/Anastomosis" title="Anastomosis">anastomosis</a>, is required for the initiation of the sexual cycle. Many ascomycetes and basidiomycetes go through a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dikaryotic" title="Dikaryotic">dikaryotic</a> stage, in which the nuclei inherited from the two parents do not combine immediately after cell fusion, but remain separate in the hyphal cells (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterokaryosis" title="Heterokaryosis">heterokaryosis</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Morelasci.jpg"><img alt="Microscopic view of numerous translucent or transparent elongated sac-like structures each containing eight spheres lined up in a row" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1040" data-file-width="1388" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Morelasci.jpg/220px-Morelasci.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Morelasci.jpg/330px-Morelasci.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Morelasci.jpg/440px-Morelasci.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Morelasci.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 8-spore <a href="/wiki/Ascus" title="Ascus">asci</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Morchella_elata" title="Morchella elata">Morchella elata</a></i>, viewed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phase_contrast_microscopy" title="Phase contrast microscopy">phase contrast microscopy</a></div></div></div>
<p>In ascomycetes, dikaryotic hyphae of the <a href="/wiki/Hymenium" title="Hymenium">hymenium</a> (the spore-bearing tissue layer) form a characteristic <i>hook</i> (crozier) at the hyphal septum. During <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell division</a>, the formation of the hook ensures proper distribution of the newly divided nuclei into the apical and basal hyphal compartments. An ascus (plural <i>asci</i>) is then formed, in which <a href="/wiki/Karyogamy" title="Karyogamy">karyogamy</a> (nuclear fusion) occurs. Asci are embedded in an <a href="/wiki/Ascocarp" title="Ascocarp">ascocarp</a>, or fruiting body. Karyogamy in the asci is followed immediately by meiosis and the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascospore" title="Ascospore">ascospores</a>. After dispersal, the ascospores may germinate and form a new haploid mycelium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sexual reproduction in basidiomycetes is similar to that of the ascomycetes. Compatible haploid hyphae fuse to produce a dikaryotic mycelium. However, the dikaryotic phase is more extensive in the basidiomycetes, often also present in the vegetatively growing mycelium. A specialized anatomical structure, called a <a href="/wiki/Clamp_connection" title="Clamp connection">clamp connection</a>, is formed at each hyphal septum. As with the structurally similar hook in the ascomycetes, the clamp connection in the basidiomycetes is required for controlled transfer of nuclei during cell division, to maintain the dikaryotic stage with two genetically different nuclei in each hyphal compartment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Basidiocarp" title="Basidiocarp">basidiocarp</a> is formed in which club-like structures known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basidia" title="Basidia">basidia</a> generate haploid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basidiospores" title="Basidiospores">basidiospores</a> after karyogamy and meiosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The most commonly known basidiocarps are mushrooms, but they may also take other forms (see <a href="#Morphology">Morphology</a> section).
</p><p>In fungi formerly classified as <a href="/wiki/Zygomycota" title="Zygomycota">Zygomycota</a>, haploid hyphae of two individuals fuse, forming a <a href="/wiki/Gametangium" title="Gametangium">gametangium</a>, a specialized cell structure that becomes a fertile <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gamete</a>-producing cell. The gametangium develops into a <a href="/wiki/Zygospore" title="Zygospore">zygospore</a>, a thick-walled spore formed by the union of gametes. When the zygospore germinates, it undergoes <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a>, generating new haploid hyphae, which may then form asexual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sporangiospore" title="Sporangiospore">sporangiospores</a>. These sporangiospores allow the fungus to rapidly disperse and germinate into new genetically identical haploid fungal mycelia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spore_dispersal">Spore dispersal</span></h3><p>
The spores of most of the researched species of fungi are transported by wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-botany.hawaii_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-botany.hawaii-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Such species often produce dry or <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobe" title="Hydrophobe">hydrophobic</a> spores that do not absorb water and are readily scattered by raindrops, for example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-botany.hawaii_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-botany.hawaii-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> In other species, both asexual and sexual spores or sporangiospores are often actively dispersed by forcible ejection from their reproductive structures. This ejection ensures exit of the spores from the reproductive structures as well as traveling through the air over long distances.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyathus_stercoreus_Fruchtk%C3%B6rper.JPG"><img alt="A brown, cup-shaped fungus with several greyish disc-shaped structures lying within" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1230" data-file-width="1641" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Cyathus_stercoreus_Fruchtk%C3%B6rper.JPG/220px-Cyathus_stercoreus_Fruchtk%C3%B6rper.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Cyathus_stercoreus_Fruchtk%C3%B6rper.JPG/330px-Cyathus_stercoreus_Fruchtk%C3%B6rper.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Cyathus_stercoreus_Fruchtk%C3%B6rper.JPG/440px-Cyathus_stercoreus_Fruchtk%C3%B6rper.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyathus_stercoreus_Fruchtk%C3%B6rper.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The bird's nest fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Cyathus_stercoreus" title="Cyathus stercoreus">Cyathus stercoreus</a></i></div></div></div><p> Specialized mechanical and physiological mechanisms, as well as spore surface structures (such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobin" title="Hydrophobin">hydrophobins</a>), enable efficient spore ejection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linder2005_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Linder2005-101">[101]</a></sup> For example, the structure of the <a href="/wiki/Ascus" title="Ascus">spore-bearing cells</a> in some ascomycete species is such that the buildup of <a href="/wiki/Osmolyte" title="Osmolyte">substances</a> affecting cell volume and fluid balance enables the explosive discharge of spores into the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trail2007_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trail2007-102">[102]</a></sup> The forcible discharge of single spores termed <i>ballistospores</i> involves formation of a small drop of water (Buller's drop), which upon contact with the spore leads to its projectile release with an initial acceleration of more than 10,000<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force">g</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pringle2005_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pringle2005-103">[103]</a></sup> the net result is that the spore is ejected 0.01–0.02<span class="nowrap"> </span>cm, sufficient distance for it to fall through the <a href="/wiki/Agaricales" title="Agaricales">gills</a> or <a href="/wiki/Polypore" title="Polypore">pores</a> into the air below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Other fungi, like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puffballs" title="Puffballs">puffballs</a>, rely on alternative mechanisms for spore release, such as external mechanical forces. The <a href="/wiki/Hydnoid_fungi" title="Hydnoid fungi">hydnoid fungi</a> (tooth fungi) produce spores on pendant, tooth-like or spine-like projections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nidulariaceae" title="Nidulariaceae">bird's nest fungi</a> use the force of falling water drops to liberate the spores from cup-shaped fruiting bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brodie1975_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brodie1975-106">[106]</a></sup> Another strategy is seen in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stinkhorns" title="Stinkhorns">stinkhorns</a>, a group of fungi with lively colors and putrid odor that attract insects to disperse their spores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Homothallism">Homothallism</span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Homothallism" title="Homothallism">homothallic</a> <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexual reproduction</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Ploidy" title="Ploidy">haploid</a> nuclei derived from the same individual fuse to form a <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">zygote</a> that can then undergo <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a>.  Homothallic fungi include species with an <i>Aspergillus</i>-like asexual stage (anamorphs) occurring in numerous different genera,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> several species of the <a href="/wiki/Ascomycota" title="Ascomycota">ascomycete</a> genus <i><a href="/wiki/Cochliobolus" title="Cochliobolus">Cochliobolus</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and the ascomycete <i>Pneumocystis jiroveccii</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The earliest mode of sexual reproduction among eukaryotes was likely homothallism, that is, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selfing" title="Selfing">self-fertile unisexual reproduction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heitman_2015_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heitman_2015-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_sexual_processes">Other sexual processes</span></h3>
<p>Besides regular sexual reproduction with meiosis, certain fungi, such as those in the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium" title="Penicillium">Penicillium</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Aspergillus" title="Aspergillus">Aspergillus</a></i>, may exchange genetic material via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasexuality" title="Parasexuality">parasexual</a> processes, initiated by anastomosis between hyphae and <a href="/wiki/Plasmogamy" title="Plasmogamy">plasmogamy</a> of fungal cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The frequency and relative importance of parasexual events is unclear and may be lower than other sexual processes. It is known to play a role in intraspecific hybridization<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furlaneto1992_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Furlaneto1992-113">[113]</a></sup> and is likely required for hybridization between species, which has been associated with major events in fungal evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schardl2003_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schardl2003-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>

none;position:absolute;text-align:center;margin:0;padding:0;top:0.000em;height:1.982em;left:2.210em;width:0.260em"><div style="font-size:100%;position:relative;top:0em;left:0em"><div style="position:relative;text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;display:block;bottom:-0.991em;transform:translateY(-50%);z-index:5"> </div></div></div><div style="font-size:100%;background-color:#ccbbaa;border-style:none none;position:absolute;text-align:center;margin:0;padding:0;top:2.203em;height:3.756em;left:3.380em;width:9.620em"><div
