<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomic_groups">Taxonomic groups</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_fungal_orders" title="List of fungal orders">List of fungal orders</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:02_01_groups_of_Fungi_(M._Piepenbring).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1842" data-file-width="2716" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/02_01_groups_of_Fungi_%28M._Piepenbring%29.png/400px-02_01_groups_of_Fungi_%28M._Piepenbring%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/02_01_groups_of_Fungi_%28M._Piepenbring%29.png/600px-02_01_groups_of_Fungi_%28M._Piepenbring%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/02_01_groups_of_Fungi_%28M._Piepenbring%29.png/800px-02_01_groups_of_Fungi_%28M._Piepenbring%29.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:02_01_groups_of_Fungi_(M._Piepenbring).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Main groups of fungi</div></div></div>
<p>The major <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a> (sometimes called divisions) of fungi have been classified mainly on the basis of characteristics of their sexual <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproductive</a> structures. As of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Fungus&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, nine major <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(evolution)" title="Lineage (evolution)">lineages</a> have been identified: Opisthosporidia, Chytridiomycota, Neocallimastigomycota, Blastocladiomycota, Zoopagomycota, Mucoromycota, Glomeromycota, Ascomycota and Basidiomycota.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naranjo‐Ortiz_&amp;_Gabaldón_2019_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naranjo‐Ortiz_&amp;_Gabaldón_2019-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Phylogenetic analysis has demonstrated that the <a href="/wiki/Microsporidia" title="Microsporidia">Microsporidia</a>, unicellular parasites of animals and protists, are fairly recent and highly derived <a href="/wiki/Endobiotic" title="Endobiotic">endobiotic</a> fungi (living within the tissue of another species).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James2006b_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-James2006b-120">[120]</a></sup> Previously considered to be "primitive" protozoa, they are now thought to be either a <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal branch</a> of the Fungi, or a <a href="/wiki/Sister_group" title="Sister group">sister group</a>–each other's closest evolutionary relative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Han_et_al._2017_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Han_et_al._2017-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Chytridiomycota" title="Chytridiomycota">Chytridiomycota</a> are commonly known as chytrids. These fungi are distributed worldwide. Chytrids and their close relatives <a href="/wiki/Neocallimastigomycota" title="Neocallimastigomycota">Neocallimastigomycota</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blastocladiomycota" title="Blastocladiomycota">Blastocladiomycota</a> (below) are the only fungi with active motility, producing <a href="/wiki/Zoospore" title="Zoospore">zoospores</a> that are capable of active movement through aqueous phases with a single <a href="/wiki/Flagellum" title="Flagellum">flagellum</a>, leading early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxonomist" title="Taxonomist">taxonomists</a> to classify them as <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>. <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">Molecular phylogenies</a>, inferred from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RRNA" title="RRNA">rRNA</a> sequences in <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a>, suggest that the Chytrids are a <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> group divergent from the other fungal phyla, consisting of four major <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a> with suggestive evidence for <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">paraphyly</a> or possibly <a href="/wiki/Polyphyly" title="Polyphyly">polyphyly</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James2006_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-James2006-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Blastocladiomycota" title="Blastocladiomycota">Blastocladiomycota</a> were previously considered a taxonomic clade within the Chytridiomycota. Molecular data and <a href="/wiki/Ultrastructure" title="Ultrastructure">ultrastructural</a> characteristics, however, place the Blastocladiomycota as a sister clade to the Zygomycota, Glomeromycota, and Dikarya (Ascomycota and Basidiomycota). The blastocladiomycetes are <a href="/wiki/Saprotrophic_nutrition" title="Saprotrophic nutrition">saprotrophs</a>, feeding on decomposing organic matter, and they are parasites of all eukaryotic groups. Unlike their close relatives, the chytrids, most of which exhibit <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle#Haplontic_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">zygotic meiosis</a>, the blastocladiomycetes undergo <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle#Haplodiplontic_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">sporic meiosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James2006b_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-James2006b-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Neocallimastigomycota" title="Neocallimastigomycota">Neocallimastigomycota</a> were earlier placed in the phylum Chytridiomycota. Members of this small phylum are <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic organisms</a>, living in the digestive system of larger herbivorous mammals and in other terrestrial and aquatic environments enriched in cellulose (e.g., domestic waste landfill sites).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lockhart2006_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lockhart2006-156">[156]</a></sup> They lack <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a> but contain <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenosome" title="Hydrogenosome">hydrogenosomes</a> of mitochondrial origin. As in the related chrytrids, neocallimastigomycetes form zoospores that are posteriorly uniflagellate or polyflagellate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hibbett2007_50-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hibbett2007-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arbuscular_mycorrhiza_microscope.jpg"><img alt="Microscopic view of a layer of translucent grayish cells, some containing small dark-color spheres" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Arbuscular_mycorrhiza_microscope.jpg/220px-Arbuscular_mycorrhiza_microscope.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Arbuscular_mycorrhiza_microscope.jpg/330px-Arbuscular_mycorrhiza_microscope.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Arbuscular_mycorrhiza_microscope.jpg/440px-Arbuscular_mycorrhiza_microscope.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arbuscular_mycorrhiza_microscope.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Arbuscular_mycorrhiza" title="Arbuscular mycorrhiza">Arbuscular mycorrhiza</a></i> seen under microscope. <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">Flax</a> root cortical cells containing paired arbuscules.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ascocarp2.png"><img alt="Cross-section of a cup-shaped structure showing locations of developing meiotic asci (upper edge of cup, left side, arrows pointing to two gray cells containing four and two small circles), sterile hyphae (upper edge of cup, right side, arrows pointing to white cells with a single small circle in them), and mature asci (upper edge of cup, pointing to two gray cells with eight small circles in them)" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="666" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Ascocarp2.png/220px-Ascocarp2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Ascocarp2.png/330px-Ascocarp2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Ascocarp2.png/440px-Ascocarp2.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ascocarp2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apothecium" title="Apothecium">apothecium</a> (the typical cup-like reproductive structure of Ascomycetes) showing sterile tissues as well as developing and mature asci.</div></div></div>
<p>Members of the <a href="/wiki/Glomeromycota" title="Glomeromycota">Glomeromycota</a> form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arbuscular_mycorrhizae" title="Arbuscular mycorrhizae">arbuscular mycorrhizae</a>, a form of mutualist <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiosis</a> wherein fungal hyphae invade plant root cells and both species benefit from the resulting increased supply of nutrients. All known Glomeromycota species reproduce asexually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Redecker2006_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Redecker2006-88">[88]</a></sup> The symbiotic association between the Glomeromycota and plants is ancient, with evidence dating to 400 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Remy1994_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Remy1994-157">[157]</a></sup> Formerly part of the <a href="/wiki/Zygomycota" title="Zygomycota">Zygomycota</a> (commonly known as 'sugar' and 'pin' molds), the Glomeromycota were elevated to phylum status in 2001 and now replace the older phylum Zygomycota.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schussler2001_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schussler2001-158">[158]</a></sup> Fungi that were placed in the Zygomycota are now being reassigned to the Glomeromycota, or the subphyla <a href="/wiki/Incertae_sedis" title="Incertae sedis">incertae sedis</a> <a href="/wiki/Mucoromycotina" title="Mucoromycotina">Mucoromycotina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kickxellomycotina" title="Kickxellomycotina">Kickxellomycotina</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Zoopagomycotina" title="Zoopagomycotina">Zoopagomycotina</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entomophthoromycotina" title="Entomophthoromycotina">Entomophthoromycotina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hibbett2007_50-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hibbett2007-50">[50]</a></sup> Some well-known examples of fungi formerly in the Zygomycota include black bread mold (<i><a href="/wiki/Rhizopus_stolonifer" title="Rhizopus stolonifer">Rhizopus stolonifer</a></i>), and <i><a href="/wiki/Pilobolus" title="Pilobolus">Pilobolus</a></i> species, capable of ejecting <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a> several meters through the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Medically relevant genera include <i><a href="/wiki/Mucor" title="Mucor">Mucor</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rhizomucor" title="Rhizomucor">Rhizomucor</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Rhizopus" title="Rhizopus">Rhizopus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walther_et_al._2019_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walther_et_al._2019-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ascomycota" title="Ascomycota">Ascomycota</a>, commonly known as sac fungi or ascomycetes, constitute the largest taxonomic group within the Eumycota.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirk_et_al.,_p._489_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kirk_et_al.,_p._489-49">[49]</a></sup> These fungi form meiotic spores called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascospore" title="Ascospore">ascospores</a>, which are enclosed in a special sac-like structure called an <a href="/wiki/Ascus" title="Ascus">ascus</a>. This phylum includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morel" title="Morel">morels</a>, a few <a href="/wiki/Mushroom" title="Mushroom">mushrooms</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuber_(genus)" title="Tuber (genus)">truffles</a>, unicellular <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeasts</a> (e.g., of the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces" title="Saccharomyces">Saccharomyces</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Kluyveromyces" title="Kluyveromyces">Kluyveromyces</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pichia" title="Pichia">Pichia</a></i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Candida_(genus)" title="Candida (genus)">Candida</a></i>), and many filamentous fungi living as saprotrophs, parasites, and mutualistic symbionts (e.g. lichens). Prominent and important genera of filamentous ascomycetes include <i><a href="/wiki/Aspergillus" title="Aspergillus">Aspergillus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium" title="Penicillium">Penicillium</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fusarium" title="Fusarium">Fusarium</a></i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claviceps" title="Claviceps">Claviceps</a></i>. Many ascomycete species have only been observed undergoing asexual reproduction (called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anamorph" title="Anamorph">anamorphic</a> species), but analysis of molecular data has often been able to identify their closest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teleomorph" title="Teleomorph">teleomorphs</a> in the Ascomycota.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Samuels2006_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Samuels2006-161">[161]</a></sup> Because the products of meiosis are retained within the sac-like ascus, ascomycetes have been used for elucidating principles of genetics and heredity (e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/Neurospora_crassa" title="Neurospora crassa">Neurospora crassa</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aramayo_et_al._2013_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aramayo_et_al._2013-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>Members of the <a href="/wiki/Basidiomycota" title="Basidiomycota">Basidiomycota</a>, commonly known as the club fungi or basidiomycetes, produce meiospores called <a href="/wiki/Basidiospore" title="Basidiospore">basidiospores</a> on club-like stalks called <a href="/wiki/Basidium" title="Basidium">basidia</a>. Most common mushrooms belong to this group, as well as <a href="/wiki/Rust_(fungus)" title="Rust (fungus)">rust</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smut_(fungus)" title="Smut (fungus)">smut fungi</a>, which are major pathogens of grains. Other important basidiomycetes include the <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> pathogen <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ustilago_maydis" title="Ustilago maydis">Ustilago maydis</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olicón-Hernández_et_al._2019_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olicón-Hernández_et_al._2019-163">[163]</a></sup> human <a href="/wiki/Commensalism" title="Commensalism">commensal</a> species of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Malassezia" title="Malassezia">Malassezia</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhimi_et_al._2020_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rhimi_et_al._2020-164">[164]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Opportunistic_infection" title="Opportunistic infection">opportunistic</a> human pathogen, <i><a href="/wiki/Cryptococcus_neoformans" title="Cryptococcus neoformans">Cryptococcus neoformans</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perfect2006_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perfect2006-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fungus-like_organisms">Fungus-like organisms</span></h3>
<p>Because of similarities in morphology and lifestyle, the <a href="/wiki/Slime_mold" title="Slime mold">slime molds</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mycetozoa" title="Mycetozoa">mycetozoans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasmodiophorid" title="Plasmodiophorid">plasmodiophorids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acrasid" title="Acrasid">acrasids</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fonticula" title="Fonticula">Fonticula</a></i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labyrinthulid" title="Labyrinthulid">labyrinthulids</a>, now in <a href="/wiki/Amoebozoa" title="Amoebozoa">Amoebozoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhizaria" title="Rhizaria">Rhizaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Excavata" title="Excavata">Excavata</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opisthokonta" title="Opisthokonta">Opisthokonta</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stramenopiles" title="Stramenopiles">Stramenopiles</a>, respectively), water molds (<a href="/wiki/Oomycete" title="Oomycete">oomycetes</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyphochytrid" title="Hyphochytrid">hyphochytrids</a> (both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stramenopiles" title="Stramenopiles">Stramenopiles</a>) were formerly classified in the kingdom Fungi, in groups like <a href="/wiki/Mastigomycotina" title="Mastigomycotina">Mastigomycotina</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gymnomycota" title="Gymnomycota">Gymnomycota</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phycomycetes" title="Phycomycetes">Phycomycetes</a>. The slime molds were studied also as <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoans</a>, leading to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambiregnal" title="Ambiregnal">ambiregnal</a>, duplicated taxonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leontyev_&amp;_Schnittler_2017_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leontyev_&amp;_Schnittler_2017-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike true fungi, the <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell walls</a> of oomycetes contain <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> and lack <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a>. Hyphochytrids have both chitin and cellulose. Slime molds lack a cell wall during the assimilative phase (except labyrinthulids, which have a wall of scales), and take in nutrients by ingestion (<a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytosis</a>, except labyrinthulids) rather than absorption (<a href="/wiki/Osmotrophy" title="Osmotrophy">osmotrophy</a>, as fungi, labyrinthulids, oomycetes and hyphochytrids). Neither water molds nor slime molds are closely related to the true fungi, and, therefore, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxonomist" title="Taxonomist">taxonomists</a> no longer group them in the kingdom Fungi. Nonetheless, studies of the oomycetes and myxomycetes are still often included in <a href="/wiki/Mycology" title="Mycology">mycology</a> textbooks and primary research literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackwell2004_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blackwell2004-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Eccrinales" title="Eccrinales">Eccrinales</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amoebidiales" title="Amoebidiales">Amoebidiales</a> are <a href="/wiki/Opisthokont" title="Opisthokont">opisthokont</a> <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>, previously thought to be zygomycete fungi. Other groups now in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opisthokonta" title="Opisthokonta">Opisthokonta</a> (e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/Corallochytrium" title="Corallochytrium">Corallochytrium</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ichthyosporea" title="Ichthyosporea">Ichthyosporea</a>) were also at given time classified as fungi. The genus <i><a href="/wiki/Blastocystis" title="Blastocystis">Blastocystis</a></i>, now in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stramenopiles" title="Stramenopiles">Stramenopiles</a>, was originally classified as a yeast. <i><a href="/wiki/Ellobiopsis" title="Ellobiopsis">Ellobiopsis</a></i>, now in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolata" title="Alveolata">Alveolata</a>, was considered a chytrid. The <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> were also included in fungi in some classifications, as the group Schizomycetes.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Rozellida" title="Rozellida">Rozellida</a> clade, including the "ex-chytrid" <i><a href="/wiki/Rozella" title="Rozella">Rozella</a></i>, is a genetically disparate group known mostly from environmental DNA sequences that is a sister group to fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naranjo‐Ortiz_&amp;_Gabaldón_2019_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Naranjo‐Ortiz_&amp;_Gabaldón_2019-153">[153]</a></sup> Members of the group that have been isolated lack the chitinous cell wall that is characteristic of fungi. Alternatively, <a href="/wiki/Rozella" title="Rozella">Rozella</a> can be classified as a basal fungal group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li_et_al_2021_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Li_et_al_2021-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleariid" title="Nucleariid">nucleariids</a> may be the next sister group to the eumycete clade, and as such could be included in an expanded fungal kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShalchianTabrizi2008_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-ShalchianTabrizi2008-144">[144]</a></sup>
Many <a href="/wiki/Actinomycetales" title="Actinomycetales">Actinomycetales</a> (<a href="/wiki/Actinomycetota" title="Actinomycetota">Actinomycetota</a>), a group with many filamentous bacteria, were also long believed to be fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PinMould_on_Peach_LowMag_Scale.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2399" data-file-width="4193" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/PinMould_on_Peach_LowMag_Scale.jpg/220px-PinMould_on_Peach_LowMag_Scale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/PinMould_on_Peach_LowMag_Scale.jpg/330px-PinMould_on_Peach_LowMag_Scale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/PinMould_on_Peach_LowMag_Scale.jpg/440px-PinMould_on_Peach_LowMag_Scale.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PinMould_on_Peach_LowMag_Scale.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pin mold decomposing a peach</div></div></div>
<p>Although often inconspicuous, fungi occur in every environment on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and play very important roles in most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a>. Along with bacteria, fungi are the major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decomposers" title="Decomposers">decomposers</a> in most terrestrial (and some aquatic) ecosystems, and therefore play a critical role in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycles" title="Biogeochemical cycles">biogeochemical cycles</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gadd2007_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gadd2007-170">[170]</a></sup> and in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_webs" title="Food webs">food webs</a>. As decomposers, they play an essential role in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutrient_cycling" title="Nutrient cycling">nutrient cycling</a>, especially as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saprotroph" title="Saprotroph">saprotrophs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbiont" title="Symbiont">symbionts</a>, degrading <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">organic matter</a> to inorganic molecules, which can then re-enter anabolic metabolic pathways in plants or other organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindahl2007_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lindahl2007-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barea2005_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barea2005-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbiosis">Symbiosis</span></h3>
<p>Many fungi have important <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbiotic" title="Symbiotic">symbiotic</a> relationships with organisms from most if not all <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">kingdoms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aanen2006_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aanen2006-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nikoh2000_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nikoh2000-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perotto1997_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perotto1997-175">[175]</a></sup> These interactions can be <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualistic</a> or antagonistic in nature, or in the case of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commensal" title="Commensal">commensal</a> fungi are of no apparent benefit or detriment to the host.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnold2003_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arnold2003-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paszkowski2006_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paszkowski2006-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hube2004_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hube2004-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="With_plants">With plants</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mycorrhiza" title="Mycorrhiza">Mycorrhizal</a> symbiosis between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">plants</a> and fungi is one of the most well-known plant–fungus associations and is of significant importance for plant growth and persistence in many ecosystems; over 90% of all plant species engage in mycorrhizal relationships with fungi and are dependent upon this relationship for survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonfante2003_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bonfante2003-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neotyphodium_coenophialum.jpg"><img alt="A microscopic view of blue-stained cells, some with dark wavy lines in them" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2700" data-file-width="1836" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Neotyphodium_coenophialum.jpg/170px-Neotyphodium_coenophialum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Neotyphodium_coenophialum.jpg/255px-Neotyphodium_coenophialum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Neotyphodium_coenophialum.jpg/340px-Neotyphodium_coenophialum.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neotyphodium_coenophialum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The dark filaments are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyphae" title="Hyphae">hyphae</a> of the endophytic fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Epichlo%C3%AB_coenophiala" title="Epichloë coenophiala">Epichloë coenophiala</a></i> in the intercellular spaces of <a href="/wiki/Festuca_arundinacea" title="Festuca arundinacea">tall fescue</a> leaf sheath tissue</div></div></div>
<p>The mycorrhizal symbiosis is ancient, dating back to at least 400 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Remy1994_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-Remy1994-157">[157]</a></sup> It often increases the plant's uptake of inorganic compounds, such as <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> from soils having low concentrations of these key plant nutrients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindahl2007_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lindahl2007-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heijden2006_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heijden2006-180">[180]</a></sup> The fungal partners may also mediate plant-to-plant transfer of carbohydrates and other nutrients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> Such mycorrhizal communities are called "common <a href="/wiki/Mycorrhizal_network" title="Mycorrhizal network">mycorrhizal networks</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Selosse2006_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Selosse2006-182">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> A special case of mycorrhiza is <a href="/wiki/Myco-heterotrophy" title="Myco-heterotrophy">myco-heterotrophy</a>, whereby the plant parasitizes the fungus, obtaining all of its nutrients from its fungal symbiont.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merckx2009_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merckx2009-184">[184]</a></sup> Some fungal species inhabit the tissues inside roots, stems, and leaves, in which case they are called endophytes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schulz2005_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schulz2005-185">[185]</a></sup> Similar to mycorrhiza, endophytic colonization by fungi may benefit both symbionts; for example, endophytes of grasses impart to their host increased resistance to herbivores and other environmental stresses and receive food and shelter from the plant in return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clay2002_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clay2002-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="With_algae_and_cyanobacteria">With algae and cyanobacteria</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lobaria_pulmonaria_010108a.jpg"><img alt="A green, leaf-like structure attached to a tree, with a pattern of ridges and depression on the bottom surface" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1939" data-file-width="2630" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Lobaria_pulmonaria_010108a.jpg/220px-Lobaria_pulmonaria_010108a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Lobaria_pulmonaria_010108a.jpg/330px-Lobaria_pulmonaria_010108a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Lobaria_pulmonaria_010108a.jpg/440px-Lobaria_pulmonaria_010108a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lobaria_pulmonaria_010108a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The lichen <i><a href="/wiki/Lobaria_pulmonaria" title="Lobaria pulmonaria">Lobaria pulmonaria</a></i>, a symbiosis of fungal, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacterial</a> species</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lichens" title="Lichens">Lichens</a> are a symbiotic relationship between fungi and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photosynthetic" title="Photosynthetic">photosynthetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>. The photosynthetic partner in the relationship is referred to in lichen terminology as a "photobiont". The fungal part of the relationship is composed mostly of various species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascomycete" title="Ascomycete">ascomycetes</a> and a few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basidiomycete" title="Basidiomycete">basidiomycetes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brodo2001_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brodo2001-187">[187]</a></sup> Lichens occur in every ecosystem on all continents, play a key role in <a href="/wiki/Soil_formation" title="Soil formation">soil formation</a> and the initiation of <a href="/wiki/Ecological_succession" title="Ecological succession">biological succession</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven2005_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raven2005-188">[188]</a></sup> and are prominent in some extreme environments, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">polar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpine_climate" title="Alpine climate">alpine</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiarid_climate" title="Semiarid climate">semiarid</a> desert regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> They are able to grow on inhospitable surfaces, including bare soil, rocks, <a href="/wiki/Bark_(botany)" title="Bark (botany)">tree bark</a>, wood, shells, barnacles and leaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Purvis2000_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Purvis2000-190">[190]</a></sup> As in <a href="/wiki/Mycorrhiza" title="Mycorrhiza">mycorrhizas</a>, the photobiont provides sugars and other carbohydrates via <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> to the fungus, while the fungus provides minerals and water to the photobiont. The functions of both symbiotic organisms are so closely intertwined that they function almost as a single organism; in most cases the resulting organism differs greatly from the individual components.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Lichenization is a common mode of nutrition for fungi; around 27% of known fungi—more than 19,400 species—are lichenized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrido-Benavent_&amp;_Pérez-Ortega_2017_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garrido-Benavent_&amp;_Pérez-Ortega_2017-192">[192]</a></sup> Characteristics common to most lichens include obtaining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_carbon" title="Organic carbon">organic carbon</a> by photosynthesis, slow growth, small size, long life, long-lasting (seasonal) <a href="/wiki/Vegetative_reproduction" title="Vegetative reproduction">vegetative reproductive</a> structures, mineral nutrition obtained largely from airborne sources, and greater tolerance of <a href="/wiki/Desiccation" title="Desiccation">desiccation</a> than most other photosynthetic organisms in the same habitat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="With_insects">With insects</span></h4>
<p>Many insects also engage in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ant-fungus_mutualism" title="Ant-fungus mutualism">mutualistic relationships</a> with fungi. Several groups of ants cultivate fungi in the order <a href="/wiki/Chaetothyriales" title="Chaetothyriales">Chaetothyriales</a> for several purposes: as a food source, as a structural component of their nests, and as a part of an ant/plant symbiosis in the <a href="/wiki/Domatium" title="Domatium">domatia</a> (tiny chambers in plants that house arthropods).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chomicki_&amp;_Renner_2017_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chomicki_&amp;_Renner_2017-194">[194]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambrosia_beetles" title="Ambrosia beetles">Ambrosia beetles</a> cultivate various species of fungi in the bark of trees that they infest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joseph_&amp;_Keyhani_2021_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joseph_&amp;_Keyhani_2021-195">[195]</a></sup> Likewise, females of several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wood_wasp" title="Wood wasp">wood wasp</a> species (genus <i><a href="/wiki/Sirex" title="Sirex">Sirex</a></i>) inject their eggs together with spores of the wood-rotting fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Amylostereum_areolatum" title="Amylostereum areolatum">Amylostereum areolatum</a></i> into the <a href="/wiki/Wood#Heartwood_and_sapwood" title="Wood">sapwood</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a> trees; the growth of the fungus provides ideal nutritional conditions for the development of the wasp larvae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> At least one species of <a href="/wiki/Stingless_bee" title="Stingless bee">stingless bee</a> has a relationship with a fungus in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Monascus" title="Monascus">Monascus</a></i>, where the larvae consume and depend on fungus transferred from old to new nests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sci-News2015_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sci-News2015-197">[197]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Termites" title="Termites">Termites</a> on the African <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savannah" title="Savannah">savannah</a> are also known to cultivate fungi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aanen2006_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aanen2006-173">[173]</a></sup> and yeasts of the genera <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Candida_(genus)" title="Candida (genus)">Candida</a></i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lachancea&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lachancea (page does not exist)">Lachancea</a></i> inhabit the <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">gut</a> of a wide range of insects, including <a href="/wiki/Neuroptera" title="Neuroptera">neuropterans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cockroach" title="Cockroach">cockroaches</a>; it is not known whether these fungi benefit their hosts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nguyen2007_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nguyen2007-198">[198]</a></sup> Fungi growing in <a href="/wiki/Coarse_woody_debris" title="Coarse woody debris">dead wood</a> are essential for <a href="/wiki/Xylophagy" title="Xylophagy">xylophagous</a> insects (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woodboring_beetles" title="Woodboring beetles">woodboring beetles</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nutritional_dynamics_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nutritional_dynamics-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulyshen_page_429-469_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ulyshen_page_429-469-200">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulyshen_page_377-427_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ulyshen_page_377-427-201">[201]</a></sup> They deliver nutrients needed by <a href="/wiki/Xylophagy" title="Xylophagy">xylophages</a> to nutritionally scarce dead wood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulyshen_page_429-469_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ulyshen_page_429-469-200">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulyshen_page_377-427_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ulyshen_page_377-427-201">[201]</a></sup> Thanks to this nutritional enrichment the larvae of the woodboring insect is able to grow and develop to adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nutritional_dynamics_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nutritional_dynamics-199">[199]</a></sup> The larvae of many families of <a href="/wiki/Fungicolous" title="Fungicolous">fungicolous</a> flies, particularly those within the superfamily <a href="/wiki/Sciaroidea" title="Sciaroidea">Sciaroidea</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Mycetophilidae" title="Mycetophilidae">Mycetophilidae</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Keroplatidae" title="Keroplatidae">Keroplatidae</a> feed on fungal fruiting bodies and sterile <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycorrhizae" title="Mycorrhizae">mycorrhizae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jakovlev_2012_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jakovlev_2012-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Parasite"></span>
<span class="anchor" id="Pathogen"></span>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Necrotroph"></span>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="As_pathogens_and_parasites">As pathogens and parasites</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aecidium_magnellanicum.jpg"><img alt="A thin brown stick positioned horizontally with roughly two dozen clustered orange-red leaves originating from a single point in the middle of the stick. These orange leaves are three to four times larger than the few other green leaves growing out of the stick, and are covered on the lower leaf surface with hundreds of tiny bumps. The background shows the green leaves and branches of neighboring shrubs." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1201" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Aecidium_magnellanicum.jpg/220px-Aecidium_magnellanicum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Aecidium_magnellanicum.jpg/330px-Aecidium_magnellanicum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Aecidium_magnellanicum.jpg/440px-Aecidium_magnellanicum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aecidium_magnellanicum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The plant pathogen <i>Puccinia magellanicum</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calafate_rust" title="Calafate rust">calafate rust</a>) causes the defect known as <a href="/wiki/Witch%27s_broom" title="Witch's broom">witch's broom</a>, seen here on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barberry" title="Barberry">barberry</a> shrub in Chile.</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Candida_Gram_stain.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Candida_Gram_stain.jpg/220px-Candida_Gram_stain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Candida_Gram_stain.jpg/330px-Candida_Gram_stain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Candida_Gram_stain.jpg/440px-Candida_Gram_stain.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Candida_Gram_stain.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gram_stain" title="Gram stain">Gram stain</a> of <i>Candida albicans</i> from a vaginal swab from a woman with <a href="/wiki/Candidiasis" title="Candidiasis">candidiasis</a>, showing hyphae, and chlamydospores, which are 2–4 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">µm</a> in diameter.</div></div></div>
<p>Many fungi are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a> on plants, animals (including humans), and other fungi. Serious pathogens of many cultivated plants causing extensive damage and losses to agriculture and forestry include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rice_blast" title="Rice blast">rice blast</a> fungus <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnaporthe_oryzae" title="Magnaporthe oryzae">Magnaporthe oryzae</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernandez_&amp;_Orth_2018_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fernandez_&amp;_Orth_2018-204">[204]</a></sup> tree pathogens such as <i><a href="/wiki/Ophiostoma_ulmi" title="Ophiostoma ulmi">Ophiostoma ulmi</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ophiostoma_novo-ulmi" title="Ophiostoma novo-ulmi">Ophiostoma novo-ulmi</a></i> causing <a href="/wiki/Dutch_elm_disease" title="Dutch elm disease">Dutch elm disease</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Santini_&amp;_Battisti_2019_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Santini_&amp;_Battisti_2019-205">[205]</a></sup> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryphonectria_parasitica" title="Cryphonectria parasitica">Cryphonectria parasitica</a></i> responsible for <a href="/wiki/Chestnut_blight" title="Chestnut blight">chestnut blight</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rigling_&amp;_Prospero_2018_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rigling_&amp;_Prospero_2018-206">[206]</a></sup> and plant pathogens in the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Fusarium" title="Fusarium">Fusarium</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ustilago" title="Ustilago">Ustilago</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Alternaria" title="Alternaria">Alternaria</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Cochliobolus" title="Cochliobolus">Cochliobolus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paszkowski2006_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Paszkowski2006-177">[177]</a></sup> Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnivorous_fungi" title="Carnivorous fungi">carnivorous fungi</a>, like <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paecilomyces_lilacinus" title="Paecilomyces lilacinus">Paecilomyces lilacinus</a></i>, are <a href="/wiki/Nematophagous_fungus" title="Nematophagous fungus">predators</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nematodes" title="Nematodes">nematodes</a>, which they capture using an array of specialized structures such as constricting rings or adhesive nets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang2007_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yang2007-207">[207]</a></sup> Many fungi that are plant pathogens, such as <i>Magnaporthe oryzae</i>, can switch from being biotrophic (parasitic on living plants) to being necrotrophic (feeding on the dead tissues of plants they have killed).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> This same principle is applied to fungi-feeding parasites, including <i><a href="/wiki/Asterotremella_albida" title="Asterotremella albida">Asterotremella albida</a></i>, which feeds on the fruit bodies of other fungi both while they are living and after they are dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some fungi can cause serious diseases in humans, several of which may be fatal if untreated. These include <a href="/wiki/Aspergillosis" title="Aspergillosis">aspergillosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Candidiasis" title="Candidiasis">candidiasis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coccidioidomycosis" title="Coccidioidomycosis">coccidioidomycosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cryptococcosis" title="Cryptococcosis">cryptococcosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Histoplasmosis" title="Histoplasmosis">histoplasmosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eumycetoma" title="Eumycetoma">mycetomas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Paracoccidioidomycosis" title="Paracoccidioidomycosis">paracoccidioidomycosis</a>. Furthermore, persons with <a href="/wiki/Immunodeficiency" title="Immunodeficiency">immuno-deficiencies</a> are particularly susceptible to disease by genera such as <i><a href="/wiki/Aspergillus" title="Aspergillus">Aspergillus</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Candida_(genus)" title="Candida (genus)">Candida</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cryptococcus_neoformans" title="Cryptococcus neoformans">Cryptoccocus</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hube2004_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hube2004-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nielsen2007_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen2007-210">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brakhage2005_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brakhage2005-211">[211]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Histoplasma" title="Histoplasma">Histoplasma</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kauffman2007_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kauffman2007-212">[212]</a></sup> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pneumocystis" title="Pneumocystis">Pneumocystis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cushion2007_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cushion2007-213">[213]</a></sup> Other fungi can attack eyes, nails, hair, and especially skin, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Dermatophyte" title="Dermatophyte">dermatophytic</a> and keratinophilic fungi, and cause local infections such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ringworm" title="Ringworm">ringworm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Athlete%27s_foot" title="Athlete's foot">athlete's foot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cook2008_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cook2008-214">[214]</a></sup> Fungal spores are also a cause of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allergies" title="Allergies">allergies</a>, and fungi from different taxonomic groups can evoke allergic reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SimonNobbe2008_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-SimonNobbe2008-215">[215]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="As_targets_of_mycoparasites">As targets of mycoparasites</span></h4>
<p>Organisms that parasitize fungi are known as <a href="/wiki/Mycoparasitism" title="Mycoparasitism">mycoparasitic</a> organisms. About 300 species of fungi and fungus-like organisms, belonging to 13 classes and 113 genera, are used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biocontrol" title="Biocontrol">biocontrol</a> agents against plant fungal diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thambugala_et_al._2020_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thambugala_et_al._2020-216">[216]</a></sup> Fungi can also act as mycoparasites or antagonists of other fungi, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Hypomyces_chrysospermus" title="Hypomyces chrysospermus">Hypomyces chrysospermus</a></i>, which grows on <a href="/wiki/Bolete" title="Bolete">bolete</a> mushrooms.
Fungi can also become the target of infection by <a href="/wiki/Mycovirus" title="Mycovirus">mycoviruses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearson_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pearson-217">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boz_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boz-218">[218]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">Communication</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_and_fungi_communication_via_mycorrhizal_networks" title="Plant and fungi communication via mycorrhizal networks">Plant and fungi communication via mycorrhizal networks</a></div>
<p>There appears to be electrical communication between fungi in word-like components according to spiking characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mycotoxins">Mycotoxins</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ergotamine3.png"><img alt="(6aR,9R)-N-((2R,5S,10aS,10bS)-5-benzyl-10b-hydroxy-2-methyl-3,6-dioxooctahydro-2H-oxazolo[3,2-a] pyrrolo[2,1-c]pyrazin-2-yl)-7-methyl-4,6,6a,7,8,9-hexahydroindolo[4,3-fg] quinoline-9-carboxamide" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1572" data-file-width="1932" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Ergotamine3.png/220px-Ergotamine3.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Ergotamine3.png/330px-Ergotamine3.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Ergotamine3.png/440px-Ergotamine3.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ergotamine3.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ergotamine" title="Ergotamine">Ergotamine</a>, a major mycotoxin produced by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claviceps" title="Claviceps">Claviceps</a></i> species, which if ingested can cause <a href="/wiki/Gangrene" title="Gangrene">gangrene</a>, convulsions, and <a href="/wiki/Hallucination" title="Hallucination">hallucinations</a></div></div></div>
<p>Many fungi produce <a href="/wiki/Biological_activity" title="Biological activity">biologically active</a> compounds, several of which are <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxic</a> to animals or plants and are therefore called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycotoxins" title="Mycotoxins">mycotoxins</a>. Of particular relevance to humans are mycotoxins produced by molds causing food spoilage, and poisonous mushrooms (see above). Particularly infamous are the lethal <a href="/wiki/Amatoxin" title="Amatoxin">amatoxins</a> in some <i><a href="/wiki/Amanita" title="Amanita">Amanita</a></i> mushrooms, and <a href="/wiki/Ergotamine" title="Ergotamine">ergot alkaloids</a>, which have a long history of causing serious epidemics of <a href="/wiki/Ergotism" title="Ergotism">ergotism</a> (St Anthony's Fire) in people consuming <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a> or related <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereals</a> contaminated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sclerotia" title="Sclerotia">sclerotia</a> of the ergot fungus, <i><a href="/wiki/Claviceps_purpurea" title="Claviceps purpurea">Claviceps purpurea</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schardl2007_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schardl2007-220">[220]</a></sup> Other notable mycotoxins include the <a href="/wiki/Aflatoxin" title="Aflatoxin">aflatoxins</a>, which are insidious <a href="/wiki/Hepatotoxicity" title="Hepatotoxicity">liver toxins</a> and highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carcinogenic" title="Carcinogenic">carcinogenic</a> metabolites produced by certain <i><a href="/wiki/Aspergillus" title="Aspergillus">Aspergillus</a></i> species often growing in or on grains and nuts consumed by humans, <a href="/wiki/Ochratoxin" title="Ochratoxin">ochratoxins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patulin" title="Patulin">patulin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Trichothecene" title="Trichothecene">trichothecenes</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/T-2_mycotoxin" title="T-2 mycotoxin">T-2 mycotoxin</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Fumonisin" title="Fumonisin">fumonisins</a>, which have significant impact on human food supplies or animal <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janik_et_al._2020_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Janik_et_al._2020-221">[221]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mycotoxins are secondary metabolites (or <a href="/wiki/Natural_product" title="Natural product">natural products</a>), and research has established the existence of biochemical pathways solely for the purpose of producing mycotoxins and other natural products in fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keller2005_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-Keller2005-38">[38]</a></sup> Mycotoxins may provide <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">fitness</a> benefits in terms of physiological adaptation, competition with other microbes and fungi, and protection from consumption (<a href="/wiki/Fungivore" title="Fungivore">fungivory</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demain2000_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-Demain2000-222">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rohlfs2007_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rohlfs2007-223">[223]</a></sup> Many fungal secondary metabolites (or derivatives) are used medically, as described under <a href="#Human_use">Human use below</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathogenic_mechanisms">Pathogenic mechanisms</span></h2>
<p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ustilago_maydis" title="Ustilago maydis">Ustilago maydis</a></i> is a pathogenic plant fungus that causes smut disease in maize and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a>. Plants have evolved efficient defense systems against pathogenic microbes such as <i>U. maydis</i>. A rapid defense reaction after pathogen attack is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidative_burst" title="Oxidative burst">oxidative burst</a> where the plant produces <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a> at the site of the attempted invasion. <i>U. maydis</i> can respond to the oxidative burst with an oxidative stress response, regulated by the gene <i><a href="/wiki/YAP1" title="YAP1">YAP1</a></i>. The response protects <i>U. maydis</i> from the host defense, and is necessary for the pathogen's virulence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Molina2007_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-Molina2007-224">[224]</a></sup> Furthermore, <i>U. maydis</i> has a well-established recombinational <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">DNA repair</a> system which acts during mitosis and meiosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kojic2006_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kojic2006-225">[225]</a></sup> The system may assist the pathogen in surviving DNA damage arising from the host plant's oxidative defensive response to infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michod2008_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michod2008-226">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Cryptococcus_neoformans" title="Cryptococcus neoformans">Cryptococcus neoformans</a></i> is an encapsulated yeast that can live in both plants and animals. <i>C.<span class="nowrap"> </span>neoformans</i> usually infects the lungs, where it is phagocytosed by <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_macrophage" title="Alveolar macrophage">alveolar macrophages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fan2005_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fan2005-227">[227]</a></sup> Some <i>C.<span class="nowrap"> </span>neoformans</i> can survive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intracellular" title="Intracellular">inside</a> macrophages, which appears to be the basis for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latency_period" title="Latency period">latency</a>, disseminated disease, and resistance to antifungal agents. One mechanism by which <i>C.<span class="nowrap"> </span>neoformans</i> survives the hostile macrophage environment is by up-regulating the expression of genes involved in the oxidative stress response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fan2005_227-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fan2005-227">[227]</a></sup> Another mechanism involves <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a>. The majority of <i>C.<span class="nowrap"> </span>neoformans</i> are mating "type a". Filaments of mating "type a" ordinarily have haploid nuclei, but they can become diploid (perhaps by endoduplication or by stimulated nuclear fusion) to form <a href="/wiki/Blastospore" title="Blastospore">blastospores</a>. The diploid nuclei of blastospores can undergo meiosis, including recombination, to form haploid basidiospores that can be dispersed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lin2005_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lin2005-228">[228]</a></sup> This process is referred to as monokaryotic fruiting. This process requires a gene called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DMC1" title="DMC1">DMC1</a></i>, which is a conserved homologue of genes <i><a href="/wiki/RecA" title="RecA">recA</a></i> in bacteria and <i><a href="/wiki/RAD51" title="RAD51">RAD51</a></i> in eukaryotes, that mediates homologous chromosome pairing during meiosis and repair of DNA double-strand breaks. Thus, <i>C.<span class="nowrap"> </span>neoformans</i> can undergo a meiosis, monokaryotic fruiting, that promotes recombinational repair in the oxidative, DNA damaging environment of the host macrophage, and the repair capability may contribute to its virulence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michod2008_226-1"><a href="#cite_note-Michod2008-226">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lin2005_228-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lin2005-228">[228]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_use">Human use</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:S_cerevisiae_under_DIC_microscopy.jpg"><img alt="Microscopic view of five spherical structures; one of the spheres is considerably smaller than the rest and attached to one of the larger spheres" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1560" data-file-width="1560" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/S_cerevisiae_under_DIC_microscopy.jpg/170px-S_cerevisiae_under_DIC_microscopy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/S_cerevisiae_under_DIC_microscopy.jpg/255px-S_cerevisiae_under_DIC_microscopy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/S_cerevisiae_under_DIC_microscopy.jpg/340px-S_cerevisiae_under_DIC_microscopy.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:S_cerevisiae_under_DIC_microscopy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</a></i> cells shown with <a href="/wiki/Differential_interference_contrast_microscopy" title="Differential interference contrast microscopy">DIC microscopy</a></div></div></div>
<p>The human use of fungi for food preparation or preservation and other purposes is extensive and has a long history. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mushroom_farming" title="Mushroom farming">Mushroom farming</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mushroom_gathering" title="Mushroom gathering">mushroom gathering</a> are large industries in many countries. The study of the historical uses and sociological impact of fungi is known as <a href="/wiki/Ethnomycology" title="Ethnomycology">ethnomycology</a>. Because of the capacity of this group to produce an enormous range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_products" title="Natural products">natural products</a> with <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial" title="Antimicrobial">antimicrobial</a> or other biological activities, many species have long been used or are being developed for industrial <a href="/wiki/Production_of_antibiotics" title="Production of antibiotics">production of antibiotics</a>, vitamins, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxol#Production" title="Taxol">anti-cancer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lovastatin" title="Lovastatin">cholesterol-lowering</a> drugs. Methods have been developed for <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> of fungi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finsham1989_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-Finsham1989-229">[229]</a></sup> enabling <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_engineering" title="Metabolic engineering">metabolic engineering</a> of fungal species. For example, genetic modification of yeast species<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baghban_et_al._2019_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baghban_et_al._2019-230">[230]</a></sup>—which are easy to grow at fast rates in large fermentation vessels—has opened up ways of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceutical</a> production that are potentially more efficient than production by the original source organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huang2008_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huang2008-231">[231]</a></sup> Fungi-based industries are sometimes considered to be a major part of a growing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioeconomy" title="Bioeconomy">bioeconomy</a>, with applications under <a href="/wiki/Research_and_development" title="Research and development">research and development</a> including use for textiles, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_meat_production" title="Environmental impact of meat production">meat</a> substitution and general fungal biotechnology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Therapeutic_uses">Therapeutic uses</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_chemotherapeutics">Modern chemotherapeutics</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Medicinal_fungi" title="Medicinal fungi">Medicinal fungi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Penicillium_rubens_(type_specimen).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="383" data-file-width="381" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Penicillium_rubens_%28type_specimen%29.png/220px-Penicillium_rubens_%28type_specimen%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Penicillium_rubens_%28type_specimen%29.png/330px-Penicillium_rubens_%28type_specimen%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d7/Penicillium_rubens_%28type_specimen%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Penicillium_rubens_(type_specimen).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The mold <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium_rubens" title="Penicillium rubens">Penicillium rubens</a></i> was the source of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penicillin_G" title="Penicillin G">penicillin G</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32973216_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32973216-237">[237]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Many species produce metabolites that are major sources of <a href="/wiki/Pharmacology" title="Pharmacology">pharmacologically</a> active drugs. 
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Antibiotics">Antibiotics</span></h5>
<p>Particularly important are the antibiotics, including the <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillins</a>, a structurally related group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%92-lactam_antibiotic" title="Β-lactam antibiotic">β-lactam antibiotics</a> that are synthesized from small <a href="/wiki/Peptide" title="Peptide">peptides</a>. Although naturally occurring penicillins such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penicillin_G" title="Penicillin G">penicillin G</a> (produced by <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium_chrysogenum" title="Penicillium chrysogenum">Penicillium chrysogenum</a></i>) have a relatively narrow spectrum of biological activity, a wide range of other penicillins can be produced by <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">chemical modification</a> of the natural penicillins. Modern penicillins are <a href="/wiki/Semisynthesis" title="Semisynthesis">semisynthetic</a> compounds, obtained initially from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> cultures, but then structurally altered for specific desirable properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brakhage2004_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brakhage2004-238">[238]</a></sup> Other antibiotics produced by fungi include: <a href="/wiki/Ciclosporin" title="Ciclosporin">ciclosporin</a>, commonly used as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunosuppressant" title="Immunosuppressant">immunosuppressant</a> during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_transplant" title="Organ transplant">transplant surgery</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Fusidic_acid" title="Fusidic acid">fusidic acid</a>, used to help control infection from <a href="/wiki/Methicillin-resistant_Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus">methicillin-resistant <i>Staphylococcus aureus</i></a> bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pan2008_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pan2008-239">[239]</a></sup> Widespread use of antibiotics for the treatment of bacterial diseases, such as <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leprosy</a>, and others began in the early 20th century and continues to date. In nature, antibiotics of fungal or bacterial origin appear to play a dual role: at high concentrations they act as chemical defense against competition with other microorganisms in species-rich environments, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhizosphere_(ecology)" title="Rhizosphere (ecology)">rhizosphere</a>, and at low concentrations as <a href="/wiki/Quorum_sensing" title="Quorum sensing">quorum-sensing</a> molecules for intra- or interspecies signaling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fajardo2008_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fajardo2008-240">[240]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h5>
<p>Other drugs produced by fungi include <a href="/wiki/Griseofulvin" title="Griseofulvin">griseofulvin</a> isolated from <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium_griseofulvum" title="Penicillium griseofulvum">Penicillium griseofulvum</a></i>, used to treat fungal infections,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loo2006_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loo2006-241">[241]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Statin" title="Statin">statins</a> (<a href="/wiki/HMG-CoA_reductase" title="HMG-CoA reductase">HMG-CoA reductase</a> inhibitors), used to inhibit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cholesterol_synthesis" title="Cholesterol synthesis">cholesterol synthesis</a>. Examples of statins found in fungi include <a href="/wiki/Mevastatin" title="Mevastatin">mevastatin</a> from <i>Penicillium citrinum</i> and <a href="/wiki/Lovastatin" title="Lovastatin">lovastatin</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/Aspergillus_terreus" title="Aspergillus terreus">Aspergillus terreus</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Pleurotus_ostreatus" title="Pleurotus ostreatus">oyster mushroom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manzoni2002_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manzoni2002-242">[242]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Psilocybin" title="Psilocybin">Psilocybin</a> from <a href="/wiki/Psilocybin_mushroom" title="Psilocybin mushroom">fungi</a> is investigated <a href="/wiki/Psilocybin_therapy" title="Psilocybin therapy">for therapeutic use</a> and appears to cause <a href="/wiki/Brain_connectivity_estimators" title="Brain connectivity estimators">global increases in brain</a> <a href="/wiki/Large-scale_brain_network" title="Large-scale brain network">network</a> <a href="/wiki/Functional_integration_(neurobiology)" title="Functional integration (neurobiology)">integration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> Fungi produce compounds that inhibit <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elMekkawy1998_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-elMekkawy1998-244">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElDine2008_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-ElDine2008-245">[245]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cancer_cells" title="Cancer cells">cancer cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hetland2008_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hetland2008-246">[246]</a></sup> Specific metabolites, such as <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide-K" title="Polysaccharide-K">polysaccharide-K</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ergotamine" title="Ergotamine">ergotamine</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta-lactam_antibiotic" title="Beta-lactam antibiotic">β-lactam antibiotics</a>, are routinely used in clinical medicine. The <a href="/wiki/Shiitake" title="Shiitake">shiitake</a> mushroom is a source of <a href="/wiki/Lentinan" title="Lentinan">lentinan</a>, a clinical drug approved for use in cancer treatments in several countries, including <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sullivan2006_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sullivan2006-247">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halpern2002_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halpern2002-248">[248]</a></sup> In Europe and Japan, <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide-K" title="Polysaccharide-K">polysaccharide-K</a> (brand name Krestin), a chemical derived from <i><a href="/wiki/Trametes_versicolor" title="Trametes versicolor">Trametes versicolor</a></i>, is an approved <a href="/wiki/Adjuvant" title="Adjuvant">adjuvant</a> for cancer therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fritz_et_al._2015_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fritz_et_al._2015-249">[249]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_medicine">Traditional medicine</span></h3>
